	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Northern gannet</h1>
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>

<p>The <b>northern gannet</b> (<i>Morus bassanus</i>) is a <a href="/wiki/Seabird" title="Seabird">seabird</a>, the largest species of the <a href="/wiki/Gannet" title="Gannet">gannet</a> family, <a href="/wiki/Sulidae" title="Sulidae">Sulidae</a>. It is native to the coasts of the Atlantic Ocean, breeding in Western Europe and Northeastern North America. The sexes are similar in appearance. The adult northern gannet has a mainly white streamlined body with a long neck, long and slender wings. It is 87–100&#160;cm (34–39&#160;in) long with a 170–180&#160;cm (67–71&#160;in) wingspan. The head and nape have a buff tinge that is more prominent in breeding season, and the wings are edged with dark brown-black feathers. The long pointed bill is blue-grey, contrasting with black bare skin around the mouth and eyes. Juveniles are mostly grey-brown, becoming increasingly white in the five years it takes them to reach maturity.
</p><p>Nesting takes place in <a href="/wiki/Bird_colony" title="Bird colony">colonies</a> on both sides of the North <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">Atlantic</a>, the largest of which are at <a href="/wiki/Bass_Rock" title="Bass Rock">Bass Rock</a> (75,000 pairs as of 2014), <a href="/wiki/St._Kilda,_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Kilda, Scotland">St. Kilda</a> (60,000 pairs as of 2013) and <a href="/wiki/Ailsa_Craig" title="Ailsa Craig">Ailsa Craig</a> (33,000 pairs as of 2014) in Scotland, <a href="/wiki/Grassholm" title="Grassholm">Grassholm</a> in Wales, and <a href="/wiki/Bonaventure_Island" title="Bonaventure Island">Bonaventure Island</a> (60,000 pairs in 2009) off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a>. Its breeding range has extended northward and eastward, colonies being established on Russia's <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a> in 1995 and <a href="/wiki/Bear_Island_(Norway)" title="Bear Island (Norway)">Bear Island</a>, southernmost island of <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>, in 2011. Colonies are mostly located on offshore islands with <a href="/wiki/Cliff" title="Cliff">cliffs</a>, from which the birds can more easily launch into the air. The northern gannet undertakes seasonal <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrations</a> and hunts for the fish that form the bulk of its diet by high-speed dives into the sea.
</p><p>The northern gannet was previously hunted for food in parts of its range, and the traditional practice still continues in the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Hebrides" title="Outer Hebrides">Outer Hebrides</a> of Scotland and the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a>. It faces few natural or man-made threats, and since its population is growing, it is considered to be a <a href="/wiki/Least-concern_species" title="Least-concern species">least-concern species</a> by the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_Conservation_of_Nature" title="International Union for Conservation of Nature">International Union for Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN). As a conspicuous and common bird, it has been mentioned in several ancient myths and legends.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Swiss naturalist <a href="/wiki/Conrad_Gessner" title="Conrad Gessner">Conrad Gessner</a> named the northern gannet as <i>Anser bassanus</i> or <i>scoticus</i> in the 16th century, noting that the Scots called it <i>solendguse</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGessner156083_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGessner156083-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> The former name was also used by the English naturalist <a href="/wiki/Francis_Willughby" title="Francis Willughby">Francis Willughby</a> in the 17th century; the species was known to him from a colony in the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a> and a stray bird that was found near <a href="/wiki/Coleshill,_Warwickshire" title="Coleshill, Warwickshire">Coleshill, Warwickshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWillughby1681328_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWillughby1681328-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> It was one of the many <a href="/wiki/Aves_in_the_10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="Aves in the 10th edition of Systema Naturae">species originally described</a> by the Swedish <a href="/wiki/Zoology" title="Zoology">zoologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in the landmark 1758 <a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">10th edition</a> of his <i><a href="/wiki/Systema_Naturae" title="Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>, where it was given the <a href="/wiki/Binomial_nomenclature" title="Binomial nomenclature">binomial</a> name <i>Pelecanus bassanus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELinnaeus1758133_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinnaeus1758133-6">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The French biologist <a href="/wiki/Mathurin_Jacques_Brisson" title="Mathurin Jacques Brisson">Brisson</a> placed it in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Sula_(bird)" class="mw-redirect" title="Sula (bird)">Sula</a></i> in 1760,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201018_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201018-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and his compatriot <a href="/wiki/Louis_Jean_Pierre_Vieillot" title="Louis Jean Pierre Vieillot">Louis Vieillot</a> moved the species to his new genus <i><a href="/wiki/Gannet" title="Gannet">Morus</a></i> in 1816.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVieillot181663_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVieillot181663-8">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> <i>Morus</i> is derived from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> <i>moros</i>, meaning "foolish", and refers to the lack of fear shown by breeding gannets and <a href="/wiki/Booby" title="Booby">boobies</a>, which enables them to be easily killed. The specific name <i>bassanus</i> is from the <a href="/wiki/Bass_Rock" title="Bass Rock">Bass Rock</a> in the Firth of Forth.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJobling201068,_260_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJobling201068,_260-9">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Ornithology" title="Ornithology">ornithologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bryan_Nelson" title="Joseph Bryan Nelson">Bryan Nelson</a> in 1978 supported the species' inclusion in <i>Sula</i> as he felt the differences in anatomy, behaviour, <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphology</a> between gannets and boobies were not sufficient to warrant separate genera.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201018_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201018-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Charles_Lucian_Bonaparte" class="mw-redirect" title="Charles Lucian Bonaparte">Charles Lucian Bonaparte</a> described the American populations as <i>Sula americana</i> in 1838,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> though the basis for distinguishing them from the European species was unclear and the name is now considered to be a synonym.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGurney190333,_289_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGurney190333,_289-11">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>"Northern gannet" has been designated as the official common name for the species by the <a href="/wiki/International_Ornithologists%27_Union" title="International Ornithologists&#39; Union">International Ornithologists' Union</a> (IOC).<sup id="cite_ref-ioclist_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ioclist-12">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> It is also known as the North Atlantic gannet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeaver201061_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeaver201061-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> <i>Gannet</i> is derived from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> <i>ganot</i>, meaning "strong or masculine", which is ultimately from the same <a href="/wiki/Old_Germanic" class="mw-redirect" title="Old Germanic">Old Germanic</a> root as <i>gander</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-OED_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> <i>Soland goose</i> and similar old names for the northern gannet such as <i>solan</i> or <i>solan goose</i> derive from a hypothetical <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Gaelic" title="Scottish Gaelic">Scottish Gaelic</a> <i>sulan</i>, itself borrowed from the <a href="/wiki/Old_Norse" title="Old Norse">Old Norse</a> <i>sula</i>. The literal meaning is "cleft stick", referring to the appearance of the conspicuous crossed black wing tips on a perched northern gannet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood1993143–44_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood1993143–44-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Old regional names such as Norfolk's "herring gant" or Yorkshire's "mackerel gant" refer to typical fish prey.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Lincolnshire's <i>gaunt</i>, although derived from the same Germanic root, usually applies to the <a href="/wiki/Great_crested_grebe" title="Great crested grebe">great crested grebe</a>, but the English writer <a href="/wiki/Richard_Hakluyt" title="Richard Hakluyt">Richard Hakluyt</a> used the term in 1600 to refer to the gannet, "a great White foule".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELockwood199368_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELockwood199368-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Young birds have been called "spotted booby" or "parliament goose", the former term referring to their plumage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201017_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201017-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The feeding habits of the gannet have led to its name being used as slang for a gluttonous person, a usage first recorded in 1929.<sup id="cite_ref-OED_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Sulidae" title="Sulidae">Sulidae</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gannet" title="Gannet">gannets</a> and boobies, appeared about 30 million years ago. Early Sulidae fossils resembled the boobies, although they were more aquatic, the gannets splitting off later, about 16 million years ago. The gannets evolved in the northern hemisphere, later colonising the southern oceans. The most ancient extant species may be the <a href="/wiki/Abbott%27s_booby" title="Abbott&#39;s booby">Abbott's booby</a>, possibly the sole survivor of an otherwise extinct separate lineage.<sup id="cite_ref-hbwfamily_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbwfamily-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> A 2011 genetic study of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_DNA" title="Nuclear DNA">nuclear</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> suggests that the ancestor of the gannets arose around 2.5 million years ago before splitting into northern and southern lineages. The latter then split into the <a href="/wiki/Cape_gannet" title="Cape gannet">Cape</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australasian_gannet" title="Australasian gannet">Australasian gannets</a> around 0.5 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The three gannets are generally considered to be separate species forming a <a href="/wiki/Superspecies" class="mw-redirect" title="Superspecies">superspecies</a>, though they have also formerly been classified as <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> of <i>Sula bassanus</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201018_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201018-7">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>An adult northern gannet has a 170–180&#160;cm (67–71&#160;in) wingspan,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201019_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201019-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> and is 87–100&#160;cm (34–39&#160;in) long and weighs 2.3–3.6&#160;kg (5.1–7.9&#160;lb),<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> making it the largest gannet<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> and the largest seabird native to the western <a href="/wiki/Palearctic" class="mw-redirect" title="Palearctic">Palearctic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977191_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977191-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The two sexes are generally of a similar size and appearance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201035_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201035-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Plumage" title="Plumage">plumage</a> is white with dark brown to black wing tips; the <a href="/wiki/Flight_feather#Primaries" title="Flight feather">primary</a> flight feathers, primary <a href="/wiki/Covert_feather" title="Covert feather">coverts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alula" title="Alula">alulae</a> are dark.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201019_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201019-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The head and neck are tinged buff-yellow, becoming much more prominent in the breeding season. Males are more deeply coloured than females.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201021_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201021-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The eyes have a light blue to light grey <a href="/wiki/Iris_(anatomy)" title="Iris (anatomy)">iris</a> surrounded by a thin black ring of bare skin.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Beak" title="Beak">beak</a> is long, strong and conical with a slight downcurve at the end and a sharp cutting edge. In adults, the beak is blue-grey with dark grey or black edges. There is a black groove running the length of the mandible that merges into the skin around the eyes. A black band of bare skin also separates the pale feathers of the forehead and throat from the bill, which gives the gannet its distinctive face markings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201020_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201020-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The four-toed feet are joined by a membrane that can vary in colour from dark grey to dark brown. There are coloured lines running along the toes that continue along up the legs. These are typically greenish-yellow in males and bluish in females and probably have a role in <a href="/wiki/Mating" title="Mating">mating</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005133_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005133-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Morus_bassanus_juv.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Morus_bassanus_juv.jpg/220px-Morus_bassanus_juv.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Morus_bassanus_juv.jpg/330px-Morus_bassanus_juv.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Morus_bassanus_juv.jpg/440px-Morus_bassanus_juv.jpg 2x" data-file-width="932" data-file-height="712" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Morus_bassanus_juv.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young bird with the front part of its body showing adult plumage.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Gannet_juvenile_RWD.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Northern_Gannet_juvenile_RWD.jpg/220px-Northern_Gannet_juvenile_RWD.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Northern_Gannet_juvenile_RWD.jpg/330px-Northern_Gannet_juvenile_RWD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Northern_Gannet_juvenile_RWD.jpg/440px-Northern_Gannet_juvenile_RWD.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1592" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Gannet_juvenile_RWD.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juveniles are dark brown.</div></div></div>
<p>Fledglings are dark grey to slate-grey with upperparts and wings finely speckled with white. There is a prominent V-shaped white area under the rump. The wing tips and tail are dark brown-black, partly tipped with white. The bill and iris are dark brown.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201024_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201024-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> They can weigh more than 4&#160;kg (8.8&#160;lb) by the time they leave the nest at about 10 weeks of age. In the second year, the bird's appearance changes depending on the different phases of moulting: they can have adult plumage at the front and continue to be brown at the rear. Gannets gradually acquire more white in subsequent seasons until they reach maturity after five years.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Northern gannets are slightly larger and thicker-billed than Cape or Australian gannets. The northern gannet has more white in the wings and an all-white tail, the other species having black tips to their tail feathers.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Individuals on the west coast of Africa could be confused with vagrant <a href="/wiki/Masked_booby" title="Masked booby">masked boobies</a>, though the latter is smaller overall, lacks the buff tinge to the head, and has a black tail.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977185,_192_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977185,_192-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> From a distance, or in poor visibility, albatrosses can be confused with northern gannets, particularly those with immature plumage that have more black on the wings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977192_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977192-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adaptations_for_diving">Adaptations for diving</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Adaptations for diving">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Northern gannets have streamlined bodies adapted for plunge-diving at high speed, including powerful neck muscles, and a spongy bone plate at the base of the bill.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The nostrils are inside the bill and can be closed to prevent water entry; the eyes are protected by strong <a href="/wiki/Nictitating_membrane" title="Nictitating membrane">nictitating membranes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor201684–86_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor201684–86-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> There are subcutaneous <a href="/wiki/Air_sac" title="Air sac">air sacs</a> in the lower body and along the sides. Other air sacs are located between the sternum and the pectoral muscles and between the <a href="/wiki/Rib" title="Rib">ribs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Intercostal_muscle" title="Intercostal muscle">intercostal muscles</a>. These sacs are connected to the lungs and are filled with air when the bird breathes in. The air can be returned to the lungs by muscle contractions.<sup id="cite_ref-Daoust_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daoust-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Feather" title="Feather">feathers</a> are waterproof, which allows the birds to spend long periods in water. A water-impermeable secretion produced by a <a href="/wiki/Sebaceous_gland" title="Sebaceous gland">sebaceous gland</a> covers the feathers and the birds spread it across their body using their beak or head.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005133_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005133-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> Individuals have a subcutaneous fat layer, dense <a href="/wiki/Down_feather" title="Down feather">down feathers</a> and tightly overlapping feathers that help them withstand low temperatures. A reduced blood flow in the webbing on their feet outside of the breeding season also helps to maintain body temperature when the birds swim.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005134_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005134-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Call">Call</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Call">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio id="mwe_player_0" controls="" preload="none" style="width:220px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="21.826371882086" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Northern_Gannet_(Morus_bassanus)_(W1CDR0001422_BD11).ogg" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/4/4a/Northern_Gannet_%28Morus_bassanus%29_%28W1CDR0001422_BD11%29.ogg/Northern_Gannet_%28Morus_bassanus%29_%28W1CDR0001422_BD11%29.ogg.mp3" type="audio/mpeg" data-title="MP3" data-shorttitle="MP3" data-transcodekey="mp3" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="136056" /><source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/4a/Northern_Gannet_%28Morus_bassanus%29_%28W1CDR0001422_BD11%29.ogg" type="audio/ogg; codecs=&quot;vorbis&quot;" data-title="Original Ogg file (226 kbps)" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-width="0" data-height="0" data-bandwidth="226198" /></audio></div>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Gannet_(Morus_bassanus)_(W1CDR0001422_BD11).ogg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Calls from <a href="/wiki/Grassholm" title="Grassholm">Grassholm</a>, Wales.</div></div></div>
<p>The northern gannet is a loud and vocal bird, particularly in the colony. Its typical call is a harsh <i>arrah-arrah</i> or <i>urrah-urrah</i>, which is emitted upon arriving or when challenging other gannets at the colony. The call is shortened to a <i>rah</i> <i>rah</i> when fishing or collecting nesting material, and lengthened to a <i>ooo-ah</i> when taking off.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201039_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201039-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> The calls of the sexes are similar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201040_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201040-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> According to Nelson northern gannets can recognize the call of their breeding partner, their chicks and birds in neighbouring nests. Individuals from outside this sphere are treated with more aggression.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005129,_310_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005129,_310-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Gannet_colonies.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Northern_Gannet_colonies.png/330px-Northern_Gannet_colonies.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Northern_Gannet_colonies.png/495px-Northern_Gannet_colonies.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a0/Northern_Gannet_colonies.png 2x" data-file-width="654" data-file-height="230" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Gannet_colonies.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Red dots show breeding colonies in the north Atlantic</div></div></div>
<p>The northern gannet's breeding range is on both sides of the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Ocean" title="Atlantic Ocean">North Atlantic</a> on coasts influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Stream" title="Gulf Stream">Gulf Stream</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Cramp1977_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cramp1977-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> There are colonies in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Saint_Lawrence" title="Gulf of Saint Lawrence">Gulf of Saint Lawrence</a> and on the islands off the east coast of Canada. They normally <a href="/wiki/Bird_nest" title="Bird nest">nest</a> in large <a href="/wiki/Bird_colony" title="Bird colony">colonies</a>, on cliffs overlooking the ocean or on small rocky islands.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> The water needs to be cool enough for <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_mackerel" title="Atlantic mackerel">Atlantic mackerel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Clupea" title="Clupea">herring</a>, which are the main food source for the northern gannet. These areas also overlie the <a href="/wiki/Continental_shelf" title="Continental shelf">continental shelf</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201042_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201042-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Northern gannet colonies can be found in the far north in regions that are very cold and stormy, and Nelson has suggested that they can survive in these regions for several reasons, including the combination of body weight and a powerful beak that allows them to capture strong muscular fish, and the ability to dive to great depths and capture prey far from the cliffs. Their fat reserves act as weight when diving and as reserves during extended periods without food.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201035_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201035-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The northern limit of their breeding area depends on the presence of waters that are free of sea ice during the breeding season. Therefore, while <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a> and Svalbard offer suitable breeding sites, the Arctic regions have summers that are too short to allow the northern gannets to lay their eggs and raise a brood, which requires between 26 and 30 weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005315_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005315-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The southern limit of their distribution mainly depends on the presence of sufficient prey.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005138_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005138-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> There is fossil evidence of northern gannets breeding on Crete in the <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding_colonies">Breeding colonies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Breeding colonies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bass_Rock_from_south_east.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Bass Rock, Scotland" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Bass_Rock_from_south_east.jpg/220px-Bass_Rock_from_south_east.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Bass_Rock_from_south_east.jpg/330px-Bass_Rock_from_south_east.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Bass_Rock_from_south_east.jpg/440px-Bass_Rock_from_south_east.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4214" data-file-height="2370" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bass_Rock_from_south_east.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bass Rock in the Firth of Forth, Scotland, is the world's largest colony.</div></div></div>
<p>Some northern gannet breeding colonies have been recorded as being located in the same place for hundreds of years. The cliffs containing the colonies appear white when seen from a distance, due to the number of nesting birds present on them. There is a written record of a colony on the island of <a href="/wiki/Lundy" title="Lundy">Lundy</a> from 1274. There were only 70 nests by 1871, and the colony finally disappeared by 1909 at the latest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201084_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201084-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> More than two-thirds of the world population breeds around the coasts of the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Colonies include:
</p>
<ul><li><b>The United Kingdom</b></li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li>Bass Rock, off the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Forth" title="Firth of Forth">Firth of Forth</a> in Scotland, first recorded in 1493.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2010282_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2010282-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In 2004, it contained more than 48,000 nests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005311_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005311-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> By 2014 this had increased to over 75,000, making it the largest colony in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcscot_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcscot-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>Northern gannets began a colony at Troup Head in <a href="/wiki/Aberdeenshire" title="Aberdeenshire">Aberdeenshire</a> in 1988,<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> and by 2014 it held an estimated 6,456 pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-jncc_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jncc-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/St_Kilda,_Scotland" title="St Kilda, Scotland">Saint Kilda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sula_Sgeir" title="Sula Sgeir">Sula Sgeir</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Hebrides" title="Hebrides">Hebrides</a>, Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The former colony was estimated at 60,290 pairs and the latter 11,230 pairs in 2013. A colony on the <a href="/wiki/Flannan_Isles" title="Flannan Isles">Flannan Isles</a> almost doubled in size to 5,280 pairs in 2013. Further south, the island of <a href="/wiki/Ailsa_Craig" title="Ailsa Craig">Ailsa Craig</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Firth_of_Clyde" title="Firth of Clyde">Firth of Clyde</a> hosted an estimated 33,226 pairs in 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-jncc_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jncc-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Ailsa Craig has been known as a colony since 1583.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201057_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201057-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>In <a href="/wiki/Shetland" title="Shetland">Shetland</a>, there were an estimated 25,580 breeding pairs at <a href="/wiki/Hermaness" title="Hermaness">Hermaness</a>, 11,786 on Noss, and 3,591 on <a href="/wiki/Fair_Isle" title="Fair Isle">Fair Isle</a> in 2013, while <a href="/wiki/Orkney" title="Orkney">Orkney</a> had an estimated 4,550 pairs at Sule Stack. <a href="/wiki/Sule_Skerry" title="Sule Skerry">Sule Skerry</a>'s breeding population rose from 57 to 1,870 pairs between 2003 and 2013.<sup id="cite_ref-jncc_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jncc-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Grassholm" title="Grassholm">Grassholm</a> off the <a href="/wiki/Pembrokeshire" title="Pembrokeshire">Pembrokeshire</a> coast in Wales had an estimated 36,011 pairs in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-jncc_47-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jncc-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Gannets began nesting here between 1820 and 1860, though never in great numbers, only exceeding 300 pairs by 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201050_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201050-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bempton_Cliffs" title="Bempton Cliffs">Bempton Cliffs</a> hosted an estimated 12,494 pairs in 2015, more than tripling in size in 12 years.<sup id="cite_ref-jncc_47-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jncc-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li><b>Ireland</b></li></ul>
<dl><dd><ul><li><a href="/wiki/Little_Skellig" title="Little Skellig">Little Skellig</a>, a small island located about 12 kilometres (7.5&#160;mi) off the <a href="/wiki/Iveragh_Peninsula" title="Iveragh Peninsula">Iveragh Peninsula</a> of <a href="/wiki/County_Kerry" title="County Kerry">County Kerry</a> is the largest colony in Ireland, hosting around 30,000 breeding pairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Known as a gannetry before 1700, the colony was exploited by local people and dwindled to 30 pairs by 1880 before rapidly increasing again.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201052_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201052-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> There are small colonies on Ireland's southern coast,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201048_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201048-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> on <a href="/wiki/Dursey_Island#Bull_Rock" title="Dursey Island">Bull Rock</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Cork" title="County Cork">County Cork</a>, <a href="/wiki/Clare_Island" title="Clare Island">Clare Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Mayo" title="County Mayo">County Mayo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saltee_Islands" title="Saltee Islands">Great Saltee Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/County_Wexford" title="County Wexford">County Wexford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDempseyO&#39;Clery20027_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDempseyO&#39;Clery20027-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Alderney</b>. Around 7,500 pairs nest on two islets—<a href="/wiki/Ortac" title="Ortac">Ortac</a> and <a href="/wiki/Les_Etacs" class="mw-redirect" title="Les Etacs">Les Etacs</a>—off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Alderney" title="Alderney">Alderney</a> in the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>, the first birds only nesting there in the 1940s. These birds migrate further south to the Mediterranean and west coast of Africa than more northerly breeding colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></dd></dl>
<ul><li><b>France</b>. The French island of Rouzic in the <a href="/wiki/Jentilez" title="Jentilez">Jentilez</a> archipelago off the coast of <a href="/wiki/Brittany" title="Brittany">Brittany</a> hosts the southernmost breeding colony of northern gannets. Established in the late 1930s, it had grown to over 11,500 breeding pairs by 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Pairs have nested sporadically with varying success along the Mediterranean coast.<sup id="cite_ref-giagnoni15_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giagnoni15-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Germany</b>. Northern gannets have reappeared on <a href="/wiki/Heligoland" title="Heligoland">Heligoland</a> in 1991 and around 680 breeding pairs have been counted in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Italy</b>. A pair raised a chick successfully for three successive seasons from 2013 to 2015 on a moored boat at <a href="/wiki/Porto_Venere" title="Porto Venere">Porto Venere</a> in northern Italy, after birds had been seen there since 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-giagnoni15_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-giagnoni15-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>The Faroe Islands</b>. <a href="/wiki/Mykines,_Faroe_Islands" title="Mykines, Faroe Islands">Mykines</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Faroe_Islands" title="Faroe Islands">Faroe Islands</a> held about 2,000 breeding pairs in 2000,<sup id="cite_ref-mykines_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mykines-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> although that had risen to 2,500 pairs by 2014.<sup id="cite_ref-ibispotts_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ibispotts-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Iceland</b>. <a href="/wiki/Eldey" title="Eldey">Eldey</a>, a small island located about 16 kilometres (9.9&#160;mi) off the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Peninsula_(Iceland)" title="Southern Peninsula (Iceland)">Reykjanesskagi Peninsula</a>, Iceland, hosting around 16,300 breeding pairs in 1962,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201072_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201072-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> and a similar number in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-bliki_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bliki-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Iceland has several small colonies along its coast,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201044,_48_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201044,_48-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> and on <a href="/wiki/Grimsey" class="mw-redirect" title="Grimsey">Grimsey</a>, around 40&#160;km (25&#160;mi) north.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201044_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201044-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Norway</b>. As of 2016, there are an estimated 6,900 breeding pairs in Norway.<sup id="cite_ref-barrett16_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barrett16-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> Northern gannets first bred in the south of the country in the mid 1940s at <a href="/wiki/Runde" title="Runde">Runde</a> in <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B8re_og_Romsdal" title="Møre og Romsdal">Møre og Romsdal</a>. In 1967 a colony was established at <a href="/wiki/Syltefjorden_(Finnmark)" title="Syltefjorden (Finnmark)">Syltefjord</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Barrett08_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett08-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> within the <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Circle" title="Arctic Circle">Arctic Circle</a> at <a href="/wiki/70th_parallel_north" title="70th parallel north">70°N</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201044_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201044-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> Colonies were also established on low islets in <a href="/wiki/Lofoten" title="Lofoten">Lofoten</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vester%C3%A5len" title="Vesterålen">Vesterålen</a>, but declined due to increasing numbers of <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagles</a>. Once rare, these were protected in 1968 and their numbers have risen. It is unclear why the eagles have not impacted on gannet numbers further north.<sup id="cite_ref-Barrett08_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barrett08-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> In 2011, gannets began breeding on <a href="/wiki/Bear_Island_(Norway)" title="Bear Island (Norway)">Bear Island</a>, southernmost island of <a href="/wiki/Svalbard" title="Svalbard">Svalbard</a>, likely due to the warming of the <a href="/wiki/Barents_Sea" title="Barents Sea">Barents Sea</a> and influx of fish. The colony numbered 52 pairs in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-barrett16_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barrett16-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Russia</b>. There are around 250 pairs in a colony at Kharlov on the <a href="/wiki/Kola_Peninsula" title="Kola Peninsula">Kola Peninsula</a> in Russia. Established in 1995, this is also thought to be due to the warming of the Barents Sea.<sup id="cite_ref-barrett16_64-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barrett16-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li><b>North America</b>. There are six breeding colonies along the coast of Canada. The <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Saint_Lawrence" title="Gulf of Saint Lawrence">Gulf of Saint Lawrence</a> hosts three colonies—<a href="/wiki/Bonaventure_Island" title="Bonaventure Island">Bonaventure Island</a>, Bird Rocks and Anticosti Island—and there are three off the eastern and southeastern coast of Newfoundland (<a href="/wiki/Cape_St._Mary%27s_Ecological_Reserve" title="Cape St. Mary&#39;s Ecological Reserve">Cape St. Mary's Ecological Reserve</a>, <a href="/wiki/Funk_Island" title="Funk Island">Funk Island</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baccalieu_Island_Ecological_Reserve" title="Baccalieu Island Ecological Reserve">Baccalieu Island Ecological Reserve</a>. Bonaventure Island off the south coast of the <a href="/wiki/Gasp%C3%A9_Peninsula" title="Gaspé Peninsula">Gaspé Peninsula</a> of <a href="/wiki/Quebec" title="Quebec">Quebec</a> is the largest colony with 60,000 nests as of 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Migration">Migration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Migration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the breeding season, adult northern gannets disperse over a wide area although they travel no more than 800 to 1,600&#160;km (500 to 1,000&#160;mi) from the breeding colony. It is not known if all birds from one colony migrate to the same over-wintering area. Many adults migrate to the west of the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a>, passing over the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> and flying over land as little as possible. Other birds follow Africa's Atlantic coastline to arrive in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Guinea" title="Gulf of Guinea">Gulf of Guinea</a>. Immature northern gannets from colonies in Canada fly to the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Mexico" title="Gulf of Mexico">Gulf of Mexico</a>, much further south than the adults.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005320_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005320-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>
The immature gannets <a href="/wiki/Bird_migration" title="Bird migration">migrate</a> southwards for great distances and have been recorded as far south as Ecuador. In their second year some birds return to the colony they were born in, where they arrive later than the mature birds. They then migrate south again at the end of the breeding season, but travel shorter distances in this second migration.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> Gannets from Alderney have been tracked since 2015 to gain better knowledge of their movements.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> One individual was found to have travelled from its colony in Alderney to Scandinavian waters, a round trip of around 2,700&#160;km (1,680&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The species has been recorded as a vagrant in many central and eastern European countries as far south and west as the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>, and also in Bermuda, Cuba, Cyprus, Egypt, Kazakhstan, <a href="/wiki/Jan_Mayen" title="Jan Mayen">Jan Mayen</a> and Syria.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Boev2009_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Boev2009-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> In February 2016, one was recorded from <a href="/wiki/Cear%C3%A1" title="Ceará">Ceará</a> in northeastern Brazil—the first sighting in the Southern Hemisphere.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Behaviour">Behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gannet_(Morus_bassana),_Belmont_-_geograph.org.uk_-_529175.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Gannet_%28Morus_bassana%29%2C_Belmont_-_geograph.org.uk_-_529175.jpg/220px-Gannet_%28Morus_bassana%29%2C_Belmont_-_geograph.org.uk_-_529175.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Gannet_%28Morus_bassana%29%2C_Belmont_-_geograph.org.uk_-_529175.jpg/330px-Gannet_%28Morus_bassana%29%2C_Belmont_-_geograph.org.uk_-_529175.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1f/Gannet_%28Morus_bassana%29%2C_Belmont_-_geograph.org.uk_-_529175.jpg 2x" data-file-width="415" data-file-height="368" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gannet_(Morus_bassana),_Belmont_-_geograph.org.uk_-_529175.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plunge-diving with wings retracted</div></div></div>
<p>The wings of the northern gannet are long and narrow and are positioned towards the front of the body, allowing efficient use of air currents when flying. Even in calm weather they can attain velocities of between 55 and 65&#160;km/h (34 and 40&#160;mph) although their flying muscles are relatively small: in other birds flying muscles make up around 20% of total weight, while in northern gannets the flying muscles are less than 13%. Despite their speed, they cannot manoeuvre in flight as well as other seabirds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201034–38_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201034–38-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Northern gannets need to warm up before flying. They also walk with difficulty and this means that they have problems getting airborne from a flat area. They take off from water by facing into the wind and strongly beating their wings. In light winds and high waves they are sometimes unable to take off and they can become beached.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201037–38_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201037–38-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Northern gannets alight on land using angled wings, fanned tail and raised feet to control their speed,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHumePearson199347_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumePearson199347-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> not always successfully, since damaged or broken wings were recorded as a frequent cause of death in adults at one colony.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005158_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005158-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Feeding">Feeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Feeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Northern gannets forage for food during the day, generally by diving at high speed into the sea. They search for food both near to their nesting sites but also further out to sea. Birds that are feeding young have been recorded searching for food up to 320&#160;km (200&#160;mi) from their nest. It has been found that 2% of birds nesting in the colony on Bass Rock search for fish at <a href="/wiki/Dogger_Bank" title="Dogger Bank">Dogger Bank</a>, between 280 and 320&#160;km (170 and 200&#160;mi) away. It is likely that they fly further than this while foraging, possibly up to double the distance; normally they fly less than 150&#160;km (93&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005320_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005320-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Some studies have found that the duration and direction of flights made while foraging for food are similar for both sexes, although there are significant differences in the search behaviour of males and females. Female northern gannets are not only more selective than males in choosing a search area: they also make longer and deeper dives and spend more time floating on the surface than males.<sup id="cite_ref-Lewis2002_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis2002-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Morus-bassanus.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Morus-bassanus.JPG/220px-Morus-bassanus.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Morus-bassanus.JPG/330px-Morus-bassanus.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Morus-bassanus.JPG/440px-Morus-bassanus.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Morus-bassanus.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Searching for fish in a zoo</div></div></div>
<p>Gannets will follow <a href="/wiki/Fishing_vessel" title="Fishing vessel">fishing boats</a> or <a href="/wiki/Cetacea" title="Cetacea">cetaceans</a> to find discarded or injured fish.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
They forage from heights of up to 70&#160;m (230&#160;ft) with no clear preference, and typically dive from between 11–60&#160;m (36–197&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-jsr_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jsr-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> They dive with their bodies straight and rigid, wings tucked close to the body but angled back, extending beyond the tail, before piercing the water like an arrow. They control the direction of the dive using their wings and tail, and fold their wings against the body just before impact.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHumePearson199349_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHumePearson199349-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> Birds can hit the water at speeds of up to 100&#160;km/h (62&#160;mph).<sup id="cite_ref-Daoust_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daoust-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> This allows them to penetrate up to 11&#160;m (36&#160;ft) below the surface,<sup id="cite_ref-JAB_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JAB-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> and they will swim down to an average 19.7&#160;m (60&#160;ft), sometimes deeper than 25&#160;m (80&#160;ft).<sup id="cite_ref-brierley_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-brierley-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The bird's subcutaneous air sacs may have a role in controlling their buoyancy.<sup id="cite_ref-Daoust_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daoust-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Gannets usually push their prey deeper into the water and capture it as they return to the surface. When a dive is successful, they swallow the fish underwater before surfacing, and never fly with the fish in their bill. Larger fish are swallowed headfirst, smaller fish are swallowed sideways or tail-first. The fish is stored in a branched bag in the throat and does not cause drag when in flight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAttenborough199846_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAttenborough199846-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
Their white colour helps other gannets to identify one of their kind and they can deduce the presence of a <a href="/wiki/Shoaling_and_schooling" title="Shoaling and schooling">shoal</a> of fish by this diving behaviour; this in turn facilitates group foraging, which makes capturing their prey easier. The colour also makes the gannet less visible to the fish underneath.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201021_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201021-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Northern gannets also forage for fish while swimming with their head under water.<sup id="cite_ref-TIN122_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TIN122-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>They eat mainly fish 2.5–30.5&#160;cm (1–12&#160;in) in length that shoal near the surface. Virtually any small fish (roughly 80–90% of their diet) or other small <a href="/wiki/Pelagic" class="mw-redirect" title="Pelagic">pelagic</a> species (largely <a href="/wiki/Squid" title="Squid">squid</a>) will be taken opportunistically. <a href="/wiki/Sardine" title="Sardine">Sardines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anchovy" title="Anchovy">anchovies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haddock" title="Haddock">haddock</a>, <a href="/wiki/Smelt_(fish)" title="Smelt (fish)">smelt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_cod" title="Atlantic cod">Atlantic cod</a> and other shoal-forming species are also eaten.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mowbray2_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mowbray2-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Breeding">Breeding</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Breeding">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Gannet_with_nest_material.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Northern_Gannet_with_nest_material.jpg/220px-Northern_Gannet_with_nest_material.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Northern_Gannet_with_nest_material.jpg/330px-Northern_Gannet_with_nest_material.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Northern_Gannet_with_nest_material.jpg/440px-Northern_Gannet_with_nest_material.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4964" data-file-height="3301" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Northern_Gannet_with_nest_material.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Transporting material for the nest</div></div></div>
<p>The oldest birds are the first to return to the northern gannet's breeding colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-BB58_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BB58-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>  Birds not of breeding age arrive a few weeks later. In general, birds first return to a colony (not uncommonly the one in which they were hatched) when they are two or three years old. Once an individual has successfully bred in a colony it will not normally change to another.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201015_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201015-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> Nesting starts in March or April.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Immature birds stay on the edges of the colony. They may even make a nest but they do not breed until they are 4 or 5 years old. Some birds of this age occupy empty nests that they will aggressively defend if they have sat on them for two or three days. If an apparently empty nest has an owner, the immature bird will leave without a struggle when the owner arrives to take possession.<sup id="cite_ref-BB58_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BB58-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The preferred nesting sites are on coastal hillsides or cliffs. If these are not available northern gannets will nest in groups on islands or flat surfaces. As they find it more difficult to take off from such locations they will often cross the area occupied by an adjacent nest causing an aggressive reaction from the sitting pair; this means that the stress levels are higher in this type of colony than in those on steeper surfaces. Notwithstanding this, nests are always built close together and otherwise ideal nesting sites will not be used if they are some distance from a colony.<sup id="cite_ref-BB58_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BB58-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> On average there are 2.3 nests per square metre (1.9 per square yard).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005328_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005328-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Both sexes fiercely defend the area around their nest.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201094_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201094-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Where space allows, the distance between nests is double the reach of an individual.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBroom2009198_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBroom2009198-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Morus_bassanus_6zz.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Morus_bassanus_6zz.jpg/220px-Morus_bassanus_6zz.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="131" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Morus_bassanus_6zz.jpg/330px-Morus_bassanus_6zz.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Morus_bassanus_6zz.jpg/440px-Morus_bassanus_6zz.jpg 2x" data-file-width="944" data-file-height="563" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Morus_bassanus_6zz.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Egg</div></div></div>
<p>Nests are made from <a href="/wiki/Algae" title="Algae">seaweed</a>, plants, earth and debris from the sea. The males usually collect the materials.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2010114_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2010114-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Nests are compact cups typically 30–60&#160;cm (12–24&#160;in) in height.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977197_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977197-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> The area which a nest occupies grows throughout the breeding season as the breeding pairs throw their excrement outside the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-BB58_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BB58-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Over years, nests can reach 2&#160;m (7&#160;ft) in height.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977197_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977197-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Northern gannets lay one egg that on average weighs 104.5 grams (3.7&#160;oz),<sup id="cite_ref-Nelson_1966_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nelson_1966-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> which is light for such a large seabird.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005150_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005150-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> The egg is around 79&#160;mm (3.1&#160;in) long by 50&#160;mm (2.0&#160;in) wide and the shell is pale blue and translucent initially before fading to a chalky white surface that is easily stained.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977197_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrampSimmons1977197-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Where two eggs are found in a nest this is the result of two females laying an egg in the same nest or one egg being stolen from another nest. Northern gannets will lay a replacement egg if the first is lost. Incubation takes 42 to 46 days, during which time the egg is surrounded by the brooding bird's warm, webbed feet. Just before hatching begins, the brooding bird releases the egg from its feet to prevent the egg from breaking under the adult's weight as the chick breaks it open. This is a frequent cause of death for chicks of birds that are breeding for the first time. The process of breaking the eggshell can take up to 36 hours.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005153,_332_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005153,_332-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The webbed feet are also used to cover the chicks, which are only rarely left alone by their parents. Chicks that are left unattended are often attacked and killed by other northern gannets.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005334_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005334-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jeune_fou_de_bassan.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Jeune_fou_de_bassan.JPG/220px-Jeune_fou_de_bassan.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Jeune_fou_de_bassan.JPG/330px-Jeune_fou_de_bassan.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Jeune_fou_de_bassan.JPG/440px-Jeune_fou_de_bassan.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1632" data-file-height="1224" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jeune_fou_de_bassan.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A downy chick</div></div></div>
<p>Newly hatched chicks are featherless and are dark blue or black in colour.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson201021_25-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson201021-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> In the second week of life they are covered in white down, replaced over the next five weeks by dark brown feathers flecked with white.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor201687_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor201687-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> Young chicks are fed regurgitated semi-digested fish by their parents, who open their mouths wide for their young to fetch the food from the back of their throats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2010121_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2010121-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Older chicks receive whole fish. Unlike the chicks of other species, northern gannet chicks do not move about the nest or flap their wings to ask for food: this reduces the likelihood that they will fall from the nest.<sup id="cite_ref-B59_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B59-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The adults feed their offspring for around 13&#160;weeks, right up until the time they leave.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2010122_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2010122-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The young birds <a href="/wiki/Fledge" title="Fledge">fledge</a> between 84 and 97 days old, departing by launching themselves off a cliff and flying—a procedure for which it is impossible to practice beforehand.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2010134_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2010134-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> If they leave the nest in bad weather they can be mortally wounded as they can be blown against the rocks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005334_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005334-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> The young birds are attacked by adults if unattended. Once they leave the nest they stay at sea learning to fish and fly, their flight skills being too poor for them to return to the breeding ledges.<sup id="cite_ref-B59_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-B59-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Northern gannets have only one brood a year. The survival rate for young birds for their first four years is 30% and the annual survival rate for adults is 91.9%. The typical lifespan after becoming adult is 17 years, and the maximum known age is 37 years 4 months 16 days.<sup id="cite_ref-BTO_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BTO-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> Gannet pairs are monogamous and may remain together over several seasons, if not for all of their lives. The pairs separate when their chicks leave the nest but they bond again the following year. Should one of the pair die, the other bird will find another mate.<sup id="cite_ref-Mowbray1_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mowbray1-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Displays">Displays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Displays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Morus_bassanus_21.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Morus_bassanus_21.jpg/220px-Morus_bassanus_21.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Morus_bassanus_21.jpg/330px-Morus_bassanus_21.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Morus_bassanus_21.jpg/440px-Morus_bassanus_21.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="955" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Morus_bassanus_21.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Fencing" or "billing", a mutual greeting gesture</div></div></div>
