<p>Northern gannets exhibit many types of aggressive behaviour while they are nesting. Confrontations normally only take place between birds of the same sex. Females will lower their heads before an aggressive male that is defending its nest: this will expose the back of the female's neck and the male will take it in its beak and expel the female from the nest. A female will not react if a male approaches a nest but it will react fiercely if another female approaches.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005326_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005326-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> The fights between males occupying nests for the first time are particularly intense. Such fights can last for up to two hours and lead to serious injuries. Birds lunge at each other and lock bills, wrestling for extended periods while neighbours peck at them. The fights are preceded by threatening gestures, which are also seen outside the breeding season. Males demonstrate ownership of a nest by gesturing towards their neighbours in a <i>bowing display</i>; their heads and beaks point down, and their wings are held up and away from the body, yet folded backwards. The male moves his head from side to side before bowing forwards.<sup id="cite_ref-BB58_84-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BB58-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Males try to attract an available female after establishing a territory. The females will fly over the colony several times before landing. Their posture, with the neck stretched out, tells the male that they are available for courtship. The male will then shake their heads in a similar way to when they are guarding their nest, but with their wings closed. Mated pairs engage in a <i>fencing display</i> when the male arrives back at the nest. The two birds stand breast to breast with wings spread and bills extended vertically. They <a href="/wiki/Fencing" title="Fencing">fence</a> and scissor with their bills rapidly, calling loudly at the same time. Fencing is interspersed with bill bowing.<sup id="cite_ref-BB58_84-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BB58-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Predators_and_parasites">Predators and parasites</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Predators and parasites">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Skua_attacking_Gannet_near_Stac_an_Armin.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Great_Skua_attacking_Gannet_near_Stac_an_Armin.jpg/220px-Great_Skua_attacking_Gannet_near_Stac_an_Armin.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="130" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Great_Skua_attacking_Gannet_near_Stac_an_Armin.jpg/330px-Great_Skua_attacking_Gannet_near_Stac_an_Armin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Great_Skua_attacking_Gannet_near_Stac_an_Armin.jpg/440px-Great_Skua_attacking_Gannet_near_Stac_an_Armin.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2998" data-file-height="1767" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Great_Skua_attacking_Gannet_near_Stac_an_Armin.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Great_skua" title="Great skua">great skua</a> robbing a gannet</div></div></div>
<p>The northern gannet is not heavily predated. The only known habitual natural predators of adults are <a href="/wiki/Bald_eagle" title="Bald eagle">bald eagles</a> and <a href="/wiki/White-tailed_eagle" title="White-tailed eagle">white-tailed eagles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Mowbray1_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mowbray1-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Predators of eggs and nestlings include the <a href="/wiki/Great_black-backed_gull" title="Great black-backed gull">great black-backed gull</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_herring_gull" title="American herring gull">American herring gull</a>, <a href="/wiki/Common_raven" title="Common raven">common ravens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stoat" title="Stoat">ermine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Red_fox" title="Red fox">red fox</a>. Attacks at sea are insignificant, though large <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pinniped" title="Pinniped">seals</a> may rarely snatch a gannet out at sea.<sup id="cite_ref-Mowbray1_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mowbray1-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kleptoparasitism" title="Kleptoparasitism">Kleptoparasitism</a> by <a href="/wiki/Skua" title="Skua">skuas</a>, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Great_skua" title="Great skua">great skua</a>, occurs at breeding sites. The skua chases its victim until it disgorges its stomach contents, providing a meal for the attacker. Skuas may catch the tip of the gannet's wing, causing it to fall into the sea, or seize the tail to tip its victim into the water. The gannet is only released when it has regurgitated its catch.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothschildClay1957255_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothschildClay1957255-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>External parasites include <a href="/wiki/Feather_louse" class="mw-redirect" title="Feather louse">feather lice</a>, although there are relatively few species and none are found on the head. As with <a href="/wiki/Grebe" title="Grebe">grebes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Loon" title="Loon">divers</a>, it may be that the short head feathers provide insufficient cover for the parasite.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothschildClay1957153_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothschildClay1957153-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> In one species, <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Michaelichus_bassani&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Michaelichus bassani (page does not exist)">Michaelichus bassani</a></i>, immature lice are found in the membranes lining the subcutaneous air-cells.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothschildClay1957226_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothschildClay1957226-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Ixodes" title="Ixodes">Ixodes</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Mite" title="Mite">mites</a> include the widespread <i><a href="/wiki/Ixodes_uriae" title="Ixodes uriae">I. uriae</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothschildClay1957231_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothschildClay1957231-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Acanthocephala" title="Acanthocephala">spiny-headed worm</a> <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Corynosoma_tunitae&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Corynosoma tunitae (page does not exist)">Corynosoma tunitae</a></i> appears to occur only in gannets and closely related seabird families such as the cormorants.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERothschildClay1957189_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERothschildClay1957189-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cestoda" title="Cestoda">tapeworm</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Tetrabothrius_bassani" title="Tetrabothrius bassani">Tetrabothrius bassani</a></i> absorbs <a href="/wiki/Toxic_heavy_metal" title="Toxic heavy metal">toxic heavy metals</a> at a higher concentration than the gannet's own tissues, with an average 12 times as much <a href="/wiki/Cadmium" title="Cadmium">cadmium</a> as the gannet's <a href="/wiki/Pectoral_muscle" class="mw-redirect" title="Pectoral muscle">pectoral muscles</a> and 7–10 times the <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> level of the bird's kidney and liver. Since levels of these toxic metals are detectable in the parasite earlier than in the host, the tapeworm might be used as an early indicator of marine pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-mendes_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mendes-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_status">Conservation status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Conservation status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gannet_Runde.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Gannet_Runde.jpg/220px-Gannet_Runde.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Gannet_Runde.jpg/330px-Gannet_Runde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Gannet_Runde.jpg/440px-Gannet_Runde.jpg 2x" data-file-width="650" data-file-height="432" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gannet_Runde.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nests among the rocks. The population of this species appears to be increasing.</div></div></div>
<p>A 2004 survey counted 45 gannet breeding colonies and some 361,000 nests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005312_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005312-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The population is apparently growing between 3% and 5% a year, although this growth is concentrated in just a few colonies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005312_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005312-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Although northern gannet populations are now stable, their numbers were once greatly reduced due to loss of <a href="/wiki/Habitat_(ecology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Habitat (ecology)">habitat</a>, removal of eggs and killing of adults for their meat and feathers. In 1939, there were 22 colonies and some 83,000 nests, which means that the populations have increased fourfold since that time.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENelson2005315_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENelson2005315-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1992, the <a href="/wiki/International_Union_for_the_Conservation_of_Nature" class="mw-redirect" title="International Union for the Conservation of Nature">International Union for the Conservation of Nature</a> (IUCN) estimated the bird's population to be some 526,000.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> After taking into account an estimate produced for <a href="/wiki/BirdLife_International" title="BirdLife International">BirdLife International</a> in 2004 of the European population, the IUCN revised its global population to between 950,000 and 1,200,000 individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-hbw_22-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hbw-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The IUCN lists northern gannets as a species of <a href="/wiki/Least_concern" class="mw-redirect" title="Least concern">least concern</a>, as they are widely distributed and as there is a large population that appears to be growing due to high breeding success, with 75% of eggs producing fledged young.<sup id="cite_ref-IUCN_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IUCN-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mowbray1_101-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mowbray1-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_culture">In culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Northern_gannet&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: In culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:92px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Leucothea_Allasseur_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Leucothea_Allasseur_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg/90px-Leucothea_Allasseur_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg" decoding="async" width="90" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Leucothea_Allasseur_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg/135px-Leucothea_Allasseur_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Leucothea_Allasseur_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg/180px-Leucothea_Allasseur_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1650" data-file-height="3666" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Leucothea_Allasseur_cour_Carree_Louvre.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Leucothea</i> by <a href="/wiki/Jean_Jules_Allasseur" class="mw-redirect" title="Jean Jules Allasseur">Jean Jules Allasseur</a> (1862)</div></div></div><p> In <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer's</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i>, the sea goddess <a href="/wiki/Leucothea" title="Leucothea">Leucothea</a> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">Λευκοθέα</span> "white goddess"), appears "in the likeness of a Gannet"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFowler2004142_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFowler2004142-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> and tells the shipwrecked <a href="/wiki/Odysseus" title="Odysseus">Odysseus</a> to discard his cloak and raft, instead offering him her veil to wind round himself which will save his life and enable him to reach land.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFlack2015155_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFlack2015155-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>
Another early reference to the gannet is in the 7th-century <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> epic poem <i><a href="/wiki/The_Seafarer_(poem)" title="The Seafarer (poem)">The Seafarer</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup></p><blockquote>
<p>There I heard naught but seething sea,<br />
Ice-cold wave, awhile a song of swan<br />
Then came to charm me gannets' pother<br />
And whimbrels trills for laughter of men,<br />
Kittiwake singing instead of mead.<br />
</p>
</blockquote>
<p>An old myth from Mykines in the Faroe Islands tells of the giant Tórur seeking mercy following defeat at the hands of Óli, the islanders' head man and magician. In return, he gave them whales, driftwood logs and a bird unique to the archipelago, on condition that the inhabitants did not laugh at his gifts. Over time, the islanders forgot their promise, and lost the whales and logs, but fearful of losing a valuable food source, they never mocked the gannets that Tórur had given them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heather_Isle_M_collecting_Guga_hunters_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1035311.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Heather_Isle_M_collecting_Guga_hunters_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1035311.jpg/220px-Heather_Isle_M_collecting_Guga_hunters_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1035311.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Heather_Isle_M_collecting_Guga_hunters_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1035311.jpg/330px-Heather_Isle_M_collecting_Guga_hunters_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1035311.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Heather_Isle_M_collecting_Guga_hunters_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1035311.jpg/440px-Heather_Isle_M_collecting_Guga_hunters_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1035311.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heather_Isle_M_collecting_Guga_hunters_-_geograph.org.uk_-_1035311.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i>Heather Isle</i> collecting guga hunters from <a href="/wiki/Sula_Sgeir" title="Sula Sgeir">Sula Sgeir</a></div></div></div>
<p>Northern gannets have long been eaten for food. Birds, mainly the young, were taken from Bass Rock for at least 350 years until 1885, when the annual cull of about 1,500 individuals finally ceased, and Shetland gannets were sold as "Highland goose" in London restaurants during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. Views of the palatability of this bird are mixed, but as well as being a food for the poor it also regularly featured in Scottish royal banquets. In Scotland gannets were traditionally salted to preserve them until they got to market, this technique being replaced by partially cooking or smoking in the era of modern transport. They are normally served roasted, although sometimes raw when pickled or dried.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The best-known site was the remote island of <a href="/wiki/St._Kilda,_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="St. Kilda, Scotland">St. Kilda</a>, where adults and eggs were taken in the spring. The fat chicks, known locally as "gugas", were harvested from the precipitous cliffs in August, just before they could fly, and thrown to waiting boats far below.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Much of the meat was salted in barrels for storage, but the rest of the bird was also used. Islanders paid their rent in feathers for stuffing pillows and furniture, the gannet stomachs were used to hold oil derived from the carcasses, and the breastbones served as lamp wicks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hunting on St. Kilda ceased in 1910, but the gannetry on Sula Sgeir is still exploited under a licence that permits 2,200 chicks to be taken each year. During the hunt, 10 men live on the island, and the cleaned birds are singed on a fire fuelled by their own oil-rich offal. The filleted birds are then taken to <a href="/wiki/Stornoway" title="Stornoway">Stornoway</a>, where each hunter receives 200 skins to give away or sell.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker200528–34-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The continuing existence of the practice of hunting and eating gannets attracts criticism in some quarters.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcscotland_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcscotland-113">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-times_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-times-114">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The island's name "Sula Sgeir" itself derives from <i>sula</i>, meaning "gannet", and the Old Norse <i>skerr</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Skerry" title="Skerry">skerry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaswell-Smith200499–101_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaswell-Smith200499–101-115">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Other sites that continued hunting into the 20th century were <a href="/wiki/Eldey" title="Eldey">Eldey</a> in Iceland, where the activity ceased in 1939, and Mykines, where small-scale culling still persists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48_48-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> About 500 young are culled for consumption each year in Mykines,<sup id="cite_ref-mykines_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mykines-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> using techniques similar to those of the Sula Sgeir hunts.<sup id="cite_ref-bbcfaroes_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbcfaroes-116">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the Bass Rock population fell to fewer than 4,000 pairs in the early 19th century, the population soon recovered once hunting ceased, and St. Kilda was harvested sustainably for hundreds of years. Elsewhere, the recovery was less complete. The Bird Rocks colony in the Gulf of St Lawrence may once have held 250,000 birds, but unchecked hunting, including for <a href="/wiki/Fish_bait" class="mw-redirect" title="Fish bait">fish bait</a>, meant that the population was only 1,000 birds by 1932, despite government protection since 1904.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48_48-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECocker2013144–48-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
